Warsinger, Mechanical Engineering, Massachusetts Institute of Technology, Cambridge, MA \n\nClimate change, degrading water resources, and economic and population growth are increasing the need for new science and technologies at the Water-Energy-Food Nexus. In enabling new and improved technologies to tackle these issues, new nanomaterials designed with systems-level thermodynamics is essential to improve efficiency, allow for new power sources, and enable applications to agriculture. Following this approach, thermodynamic design of water treatment membrane technologies such as reverse osmosis (RO) and membrane distillation (MD) leads to innovations with superhydrophobic nanostructured surfaces for enhanced heat transfer, time-varying \u201cbatch\u201d cycles, new system configurations, and optimal use of waste heat can more than double system efficiencies. Furthermore, optimization of heat and mass transfer and chemical thermodynamics in these technologies allows for nanofabricated membranes with superior flux and fouling resistance, including graphene oxide multilayer membranes. These approaches not only yield significant improvements in water treatment and associated energy use, but also in providing water and energy needs for food applications, such as pumping energy for agricultural ground water, or using phase-change thermal storage to enable refrigeration of food despite intermittent power in the developing world.\n\nAvailable on nanoHUB.org https://nanohub.org/resources/26190\n\nReferences:\n\u2022 World Economic Forum, Geneva, Switzerland, \"Global risks 2015 10th edition,\" 2015.\n\u2022 D. Clea Kolster will provide quantitative insights into the myriad of risks associated with CCS and will introduce pragmatic, cost-minimizing solutions to the development of large-scale CCS.\n\nSpeaker Biography:\n\nClea is a Consultant at E3 (Energy & Environmental Economics), where she works on evaluating implications of electricity generation policies and investments on energy market dynamics by using and developing optimization models.\n\nPrior to joining E3, Clea completed her PhD in June 2018 within the Centre for Process Systems Engineering & Centre for Environmental Policy at Imperial College London, where she focused on economic, process and reservoir modelling for evaluating large-scale deployment of CCS. Scientists study these signals to find out how they can cause a cell's particular behaviour, and how they can be manipulated with drugs to treat diseases such as cancer. \n\nFor decades, a major way scientists have studied cancer in the lab has been by growing a single layer of cancer cells in a dish, doing experiments on those cells and observing the outcome. Although this approach has taught us an unbelievable amount about how both healthy and diseased cells work, the reality of cells in an environment like the human body is much more complex.\n\nThe microenvironment inside a tumour contains more than just cancer cells. Many different kinds of healthy cells are present in the tumour as well, and can have their healthy function hijacked by the cancer cells. For instance, fibroblasts inside the tumour can be co-opted by the cancer cells in order to grow a support structure for the cancer cells to grow on. There are all kinds of different immune cells inside a tumour as well, which try to attack the tumour but can end up being hijacked themselves as well and end up helping the cancer grow. \n\nInside the microenvironment of the tumour, all these different cell types, both healthy and diseased, are constantly communicating with one another. We call this complex tangle of communication between different types of cells Heterocellular Signalling. We know very little about how this heterocellular signalling works inside a tumour, because it's just been too difficult to study this dynamic behaviour.\n\nBut new technological advances are allowing us to finally figure out how all these different cell types are communicating with one another. This will teach us lots about how a tumour grows and spreads, and will hopefully allow us to find new ways of treating cancer.\n\nFor more information, see:\n- Chris Tape's latest review in Trends in Cancer on heterocellular signalling: http://dx.doi.org/10.1016/j.trecan.2016.12.004\n- His blogpost partnering the review: http://christape.com/blog/2016/12/5/heterocellular-emergence\n- Our video on Kharis", "Endang Sri Maruti", "Vincentius Mauk", "dan Heppy Lismayanti"], "defaultAudioLanguage": "id"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT29M21S"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.is.2016.12.004", "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.is.2016.12.004"}, {"items": [{"id": "ugYsQFhegfQ", "statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "viewCount": "62", "likeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "favoriteCount": "0"}, "snippet": {"publishedAt": "2017-05-18T22:02:27Z", "channelId": "UCaEXzhM-yG3UI4XTnG51wUw", "channelTitle": "Work Wellness Institute", "description": "In these series many ideas on how to keep older workers in the workforce have been put forward.  In this seminar we want to provide an overview of the evidence on the effectiveness of interventions aimed at promoting work participation in older workers. In our study, we followed a systematic review process and a best evidence synthesis that ranked evidence as strong, moderate, limited, or insufficient. The literature was searched from inception to March 2014. Evidence from 14 studies were synthesized into different intervention categories. There was moderate evidence that work participation was improved by multi-component interventions encompassing at least two of three components (health service delivery, coordination of services, and work modifications). There was not enough evidence to recommend other interventions. Conclusions: Although there is a vast body of research on work participation of older workers, there are only a few high quality intervention studies aimed at improving work participation in this population. We recommend that multi-component interventions could be considered for implementation by practitioners to help improve work participation in older workers. Practical applications: With a moderate level of evidence, multi-component interventions could be considered for use in practice if practitioners deem it suitable for their setting. There is not enough evidence to recommend exercise interventions, pharmaceutical interventions, different types of surgeries, patient education or work accommodation alone to improve work participation. Prof. Dr. Amporn Wiwegweaw\n\nAbstract\nThe Mississippi River is recognized as an important geographic barrier to gene flow and generates species in North America. The yellow-bellied kingsnake, Lampropeltis calligaster, is widely distributed in several areas in North America. It consists of three subspecies: L. c. calligaster, L. c. rhombomaculata and L. c. occipitolineata, which differ morphologically and geographically (distinct ecological niches). The former two subspecies are primarily found in western (grasslands) and eastern (forests) sides of the Mississippi River regions, respectively, and they were historically isolated by this river, while the third is restricted to South Florida peninsular. In this study, the researchers utilized phylogeographic analyses to examine timing and mode of speciation and causes of current lineage diversification in the L. calligaster complex. Gene trees constructed by mtDNA (Cytochrome b) and nDNA (CLLAT, NT3, PRLR and VIM56 loci) sequences demonstrated that 98% of individuals were clearly grouped into 3 lineages: western, eastern and South Florida lineages. Divergence dating and historical demography analyses revealed that three subspecies originated during the Pleistocene epoch, followed by range expansion and increasing population size over the last 100,000 years. Additionally, the result from a Mantel test showed that the pattern of lineage diversification is strongly associated with ecological divergence occurred on each side of the river. This result supports the hypothesis that ecological transition due to climate change during the Pleistocene is a causal factor driving L. calligaster diversification. Migrate tests detected symmetric gene flow with equal rate of migration between eastern and western populations, indicating that speciation and current diversification in this species groups is likely due to the effect of ecological transition rather than historical riverine vicariance. Moreover, ecological niche modeling predicted that the future ranges of eastern and South Florida kingsnake may be decreased due to urbanization and climate change. This prediction provides preliminary information to assist conservation planning and management for L. calligaster. in the future. \n\nReferences:\nMcKelvy, A.D., Burbrink, F.T. 2017. In fact, we must go beyond the typical conversation around informational privacy (who can see what) and focus on a broader range of social privacy issues such as interactional (e.g., pressure to interact), psychological (e.g., impression management), or physical (e.g., concern others will come join me). Based on empirical research, this talk investigates various privacy issues around technology use and how addressing them is tied to understanding individual differences in user perceptions, preferences, and mental models. We explore how these differences shape technology use and non-use for a range of technologies. For example, differing mental models that underlie generational differences for preferred interaction with the internet of things, individual traits (e.g., an FYI communication style) that shape social media usage, and human-algorithm dynamics that change over time and impact work effectiveness. This talk discusses the challenges in designing for users who, in actuality, have diverse preferences and needs and concludes by providing design principles that can be used when considering your end-user.", "title": "Xinru Page : Privacy is Not \"One-Size-Fits-All\": Addressing Individual Privacy Needs (April 2019)", "tags": ["BostonCHI", "Privacy", "Design", "Xinru Page", "Bentley", "2019"]}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT1H24M5S"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.im.2016.09.006", "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.im.2016.09.006"}, {"items": [{"id": "1-rkn8CGmis", "statistics": {"dislikeCount": "3", "viewCount": "5115", "likeCount": "298", "commentCount": "23", "favoriteCount": "0"}, "snippet": {"publishedAt": "2020-05-02T01:00:00Z", "channelId": "UCPQS5BOA_GLQKCc07ZCOjJg", "channelTitle": "Michelle Kenway", "description": "This Physiotherapy video guides you through 4 exercises to avoid that can increase the risk of spinal fracture if you have high fracture with osteoporosis. Some of these exercises to avoid are commonplace in fitness centers and some exercise classes (e.g. Yoga and Pilates). Knowing how to modify potentially unsafe exercises can help you exercise safely and reduce the risk of spinal fracture with osteoporosis.\n\nWebsite: https://www.pelvicexercises.com.au\n\nExercise 1: Forward Bends\nExercises that involve bending forwards can increase the risk of having a spinal wedge or compression fracture with severe osteoporosis (Vertebral T-score less than \u22122.5 SD). A spinal compression fracture occurs when the vertebra are soft and brittle. Bending forwards compresses the vertebra which can cause them to become squashed and fracture with severely decreased bone density. Exercises such as abdominal curls and deep forward bend in Yoga compress the vertebra together. These exercises are easily modified with to reduce the risk of fracture.\n\nExercise 2: Loaded Forward Bends\nLoaded forward bends further increase the risk of spinal wedge fracture and are therefore unsafe osteoporosis exercises. The risk of osteoporosis fracture increases the further forward you bend and the heavier the weight you lift.\n\nExercise 3:  Spinal Rotation\nYour spinal vertebra rotate during normal movement however some spinal rotation exercises can increase the risk of spinal fracture. Parts of the vertebra are forced against each other when the spine rotates to extreme ranges. Examples of extreme range spinal twisting exercises to avoid with severe osteoporosis include full range lumbar rotation exercises taking the knees right across to the ground and abdominal curl exercises that are combined with spinal rotations.\n\nExercise 4: Direct Spinal Loading\nSome gym exercises for the legs and back involve placing a weighted bar at the top of the spine and across the shoulders. This force on the upper spine travels down through the vertebra and the load can compress soft brittle vertebrae causing a compression fracture. These unsafe osteoporosis exercises are readily modified \n\nIf you're currently doing any of these exercises and you have a high risk of spinal fracture with osteoporosis it's worth stopping these exercises now and discussing whether they're appropriate for your condition with your health professional.\n\n#osteoporosisexercises  #osteoporosis #spinalfracture\n\n\nReferences\nBeck BR1, Daly RM2, Singh MA3, Taaffe DR4. If you are planning to listen a lot, you could try using a bone conduction headphone, or a standard speaker instead of an earphone.\r\n\r\nYou can find other Wikipedia audio articles too at:\r\nhttps://www.youtube.com/channel/UCuKfABj2eGyjH3ntPxp4YeQ\r\n\r\nYou can upload your own Wikipedia articles through:\r\nhttps://github.com/nodef/wikipedia-tts\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\"The only true wisdom is in knowing you know nothing.\"\r\n- Socrates\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nSUMMARY\r\n=======\r\nWilliam Whewell  ( HEW-\u0259l; 24 May 1794 \u2013 6 March 1866) was an English polymath, scientist, Anglican priest, philosopher, theologian, and historian of science. He was Master of Trinity College, Cambridge.  In his time as a student there, he achieved distinction in both poetry and mathematics.\nWhat is most often remarked about Whewell is the breadth of his endeavours.  In a time of increasing specialisation, Whewell appears as a vestige of an earlier era when natural philosophers dabbled in a bit of everything. He researched ocean tides (for which he won the Royal Medal), published work in the disciplines of mechanics, physics, geology, astronomy, and economics, while also finding the time to compose poetry, author a Bridgewater Treatise, translate the works of Goethe, and write sermons and theological tracts.  In mathematics, Whewell introduced what is now called the Whewell equation, an equation defining the shape of a curve without reference to an arbitrarily chosen coordinate system.\nOne of Whewell's greatest gifts to science was his wordsmithing.  He often corresponded with many in his field and helped them come up with new terms for their discoveries.  Whewell contributed the terms scientist, physicist, linguistics, consilience, catastrophism, uniformitarianism, and astigmatism amongst others; Whewell suggested the terms electrode, ion, dielectric, anode, and cathode to Michael Faraday.Whewell died in Cambridge in 1866 as a result of a fall from his horse.", "title": "William Whewell | Wikipedia audio article", "tags": ["william whewell", "1794 births", "1866 deaths", "ac with 14 elements", "anglican philosophers", "english economists", "english philosophers", "english scientists", "fellows of the royal society", "giftedness", "historians of science", "members of the club", "philosophers of science", "royal medal winners", "second wranglers", "wikipedia audio article", "learning by listening", "improves your listening skills", "learn while on the move", "reduce eye strain", "text to speech"], "defaultLanguage": "en", "defaultAudioLanguage": "en"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT21M3S"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.im.2016.10.001", "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.im.2016.10.001"}, {"items": [{"id": "T6Gzue-Lxqs", "statistics": {"dislikeCount": "1", "viewCount": "3190", "likeCount": "19", "commentCount": "4", "favoriteCount": "0"}, "snippet": {"publishedAt": "2016-11-28T02:22:03Z", "channelTitle": "EarthByte", "description": "EarthByte, The University of Sydney\nMatthews et al. \u0434\u0443\u043c\u0430\u0435\u0442\u0435 \u043f\u043e \u044d\u0442\u043e\u043c\u0443 \u043f\u043e\u0432\u043e\u0434\u0443?\n\n\nImpact of Intermittent Fasting on Health and Disease Processes\nMark P Mattson 1, Valter D Longo 2, Michelle Harvie 3\nAffiliations expand\nPMID: 27810402 PMCID: PMC5411330 DOI: 10.1016/j.arr.2016.10.005\nAbstract\nHumans in modern societies typically consume food at least three times daily, while laboratory animals are fed ad libitum. Overconsumption of food with such eating patterns often leads to metabolic morbidities (insulin resistance, excessive accumulation of visceral fat, etc.), particularly when associated with a sedentary lifestyle. Because animals, including humans, evolved in environments where food was relatively scarce, they developed numerous adaptations that enabled them to function at a high level, both physically and cognitively, when in a food-deprived/fasted state. Intermittent fasting (IF) encompasses eating patterns in which individuals go extended time periods (e.g., 16-48h) with little or no energy intake, with intervening periods of normal food intake, on a recurring basis. We use the term periodic fasting (PF) to refer to IF with periods of fasting or fasting mimicking diets lasting from 2 to as many as 21 or more days. In laboratory rats and mice IF and PF have profound beneficial effects on many different indices of health and, importantly, can counteract disease processes and improve functional outcome in experimental models of a wide range of age-related disorders including diabetes, cardiovascular disease, cancers and neurological disorders such as Alzheimer's disease Parkinson's disease and stroke. Studies of IF (e.g., 60% energy restriction on 2days per week or every other day), PF (e.g., a 5day diet providing 750-1100kcal) and time-restricted feeding (TRF; limiting the daily period of food intake to 8h or less) in normal and overweight human subjects have demonstrated efficacy for weight loss and improvements in multiple health indicators including insulin resistance and reductions in risk factors for cardiovascular disease. The cellular and molecular mechanisms by which IF improves health and counteracts disease processes involve activation of adaptive cellular stress response signaling pathways that enhance mitochondrial health, DNA repair and autophagy. PF also promotes stem cell-based regeneration as well as long-lasting metabolic effects. Randomized controlled clinical trials of IF versus PF and isoenergetic continuous energy restriction in human subjects will be required to establish the efficacy of IF in improving general health, and preventing and managing major diseases of aging", "title": "\u0417\u0430\u043f\u0438\u0448\u0438\u0442\u0435\u0441\u044c \u0432 \u0433\u0440\u0443\u043f\u043f\u0443 \u043f\u043e \u0433\u043e\u043b\u043e\u0434\u0430\u043d\u0438\u044e: 5-10-15-20 \u0434\u043d\u0435\u0439. \u041f\u0441\u0438\u0445\u043e\u0441\u043e\u043c\u0430\u0442\u0438\u043a\u0430 \u0441 \u043a\u043e\u0443\u0447\u0435\u043c.", "tags": ["\u0433\u043e\u043b\u043e\u0434\u0430\u043d\u0438\u0435", "\u0430\u043b\u043b\u0435\u0440\u0433\u0438\u0438", "\u043a\u0430\u043d\u0434\u0438\u0434\u0430", "\u043a\u043e\u0443\u0447\u0438\u043d\u0433", "\u0438\u0440\u0438\u043d\u0430\u0448\u0443\u043b\u044c\u043a\u0438\u043d\u0430", "\u043f\u0441\u0438\u0445\u043e\u0441\u043e\u043c\u0430\u0442\u0438\u043a\u0430", "5\u0434\u043d\u0435\u0439\u0433\u043e\u043b\u043e\u0434\u0430\u043d\u0438\u044f", "10\u0434\u043d\u0435\u0439\u0433\u043e\u043b\u043e\u0434\u0430\u043d\u0438\u044f", "20\u0434\u043d\u0435\u0439\u0433\u043e\u043b\u043e\u0434\u0430\u043d\u0438\u044f"], "defaultAudioLanguage": "en"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT13M55S"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.im.2016.10.005", "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.im.2016.10.005"}, {"items": [{"id": "xarRy_05r_s", "statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "viewCount": "3", "likeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "favoriteCount": "0"}, "snippet": {"publishedAt": "2020-06-28T09:13:39Z", "channelTitle": "Jens Nielson", "description": "This video is intended for an audience of college-students. Periodic fasting and Intermittent fasting. What are the benefits of fasting and is it sustainable?\nFirstly, the most popular version of Periodic fasting is Time-Restricted feeding. This is where you fast for 16 hours and eat within a window of 8 hours. This looks like eating your evening meal by 6pm at night, and not eating again until 10 am the next morning and then eating your normal diet, preferably modelled on the Mediterranean diet throughout that next eight hours. Dr Valter Longo recommends that 12-13 hours of fasting is safe and manageable and has found that people do better with this way of eating - it also prevents gall stone formation that the longer hours of fasting may promote.\nThe other version of periodic fasting incorporates for example, a 5 day diet providing 750-1100kcal and this is called the fasting mimicking diet. It is suggested by Dr Valter Longo that this is an effective way of fasting and is great to do 2-3 times a year and the diet his fasting diet is a vegan diet made up of fat 45%, Carbs 45% and Protein 10%. He has a book that you can check out called The Longevity Diet. \nThe second is Intermittent fasting (IF) that encompasses eating patterns in which individuals go extended time periods (e.g., 16-48h) with little or no energy intake, with intervening periods of normal food intake, on a recurring basis. \nHealth Benefits\nLifestyle behaviours such as, intermittent fasting and caloric restriction, and  exercise trigger the activity of sirtuins, restoring normal gene regulation, resetting the cell's activity, and slowing the aging process. There are a number of disorders this affects such as for several human disorders, including infections and inflammatory diseases, neurodegeneration, metabolic and cardiovascular diseases, obesity and cancer. \nDr Valter Longo suggest that Fasting \u2013 cleans house in the body and is a powerful way to treat disease and prevent cancer.  His research also suggests that the Fasting Mimicking Diet and Cancer treatment (drug therapy) in combination are very effective in providing a good outcome for the patient.\nSustainability\nModifiable lifestyle adjustments such as smoking cessation, increasing movement, ensuring proper body weight all assist in reducing the risk of cardiovascular disease.
